### LaTeX Source for the Methodology Section

Copy and paste the following code directly into your `.tex` document (right after your Introduction). Make sure to save your images as `figure1.jpg` and `figure2.jpg` in your working directory.

```latex
% ---------------------------------------------------------
% Start of Methodology Section
% ---------------------------------------------------------
\section{Methodology: The ``Virtual Research Group'' Workflow}
\label{sec:methodology}

Translating continuous quantum many-body theory into discrete, high-performance array operations requires tacit computational knowledge that is rarely explicit in physical literature. To navigate this, we structured our multi-agent LLM pipeline to mimic the pedagogical and hierarchical dynamics of a traditional academic research group. As illustrated in Fig.~\ref{fig:workflow}, the workflow partitions algorithm development into three distinct LLM roles, supervised by a human Principal Investigator (PI).

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    % Ensure your image file is named appropriately, e.g., figure1.jpg
    \includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figure1.jpg} 
    \caption{\textbf{The ``Paper to Program'' Multi-Agent Workflow.} The development process mimics a virtual research group. \textbf{(A)} The theoretical source material. \textbf{(B)} A zero-shot translation by LLM-0 (``Junior Theorist'') yields a flawed initial draft, characterized by hallucinated tensor indices and severe memory scaling bottlenecks. \textbf{(C)} The crucial intermediate step: LLM-1 (``Senior Postdoc'') reviews and corrects the draft, generating a mathematically rigorous formal specification. This stage enforces universal index conventions and explicit memory optimizations (e.g., \texttt{optimize=True}). \textbf{(D)} Constrained by this strict blueprint, LLM-2 (``Coder'') reliably generates scalable Python code. The Human PI remains in the loop strictly for high-level physics verification and pedagogical feedback.}
    \label{fig:workflow}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Stage 1: Theory Extraction (LLM-0 / The ``Junior Theorist'')}
The workflow begins by providing the source literature—in this case, the comprehensive review of MPS and DMRG by Schollw\"ock (2011)—to the first foundation model, LLM-0 (Kimi 2.5). The objective of this agent is to extract the fundamental theoretical equations, such as Matrix Product Operator (MPO) representations, QR/SVD canonicalization routines, and effective Hamiltonian contractions, and translate them into an initial LaTeX draft.

Much like a junior graduate student, LLM-0 succeeds at extracting the broad mathematical strokes but frequently fails to account for computational realities. As depicted in Fig.~\ref{fig:workflow}B, direct extraction often results in messy pseudo-code containing hallucinatory index mappings, missing library prefixes (e.g., \texttt{numpy}), and a failure to optimize tensor contraction paths, which would immediately lead to fatal $\mathcal{O}(D^6)$ memory blowups if executed.

\subsection{Stage 2: Expert Specification (LLM-1 / The ``Senior Postdoc'')}
The most critical innovation of this workflow is Stage 2. We expressly forbid the direct translation of the Stage 1 draft into Python. Instead, the draft is passed to an ``expert reviewer'' model, LLM-1 (Gemini 3.1), tasked with line-by-line verification for computational and coding readiness. 

LLM-1 acts as the senior specifying agent. It autonomously identifies algorithmic ambiguities and injects tacit physics domain knowledge to create a rigorous formal blueprint (Fig.~\ref{fig:workflow}C). Key optimizations introduced at this stage include:
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Universal Index Conventions:} Explicitly defining a rigid nomenclature for tensor legs (e.g., \texttt{b/B} for MPO bonds, \texttt{x/X} for bra bonds, \texttt{y/Y} for ket bonds) to entirely eliminate \texttt{numpy.einsum} broadcasting errors.
    \item \textbf{Matrix-Free Scalability:} Replacing dense matrix constructions with iterative \texttt{scipy.sparse.linalg.LinearOperator} implementations, ensuring the effective Hamiltonian application scales strictly as $\mathcal{O}(D^3)$.
    \item \textbf{Memory Management:} Enforcing the use of \texttt{np.tensordot} for gauge shifting to leverage optimized BLAS routines, and differentiating between memory views and deep copies in NumPy array reshaping.
\end{itemize}
The output of this stage is a flawlessly compiled, highly detailed LaTeX document. This document serves as a universal, model-agnostic Application Programming Interface (API) for the final coding stage.

\subsection{Stage 3: Code Implementation \& HITL Mentorship (LLM-2 \& Human PI)}
In the final stage, the formal LaTeX specification is passed to the implementation agent, LLM-2 (e.g., GPT, Claude, or Kimi Agent). Because the LaTeX blueprint mathematically constrains the tensor shapes and contraction paths, LLM-2 is relieved of spatial reasoning tasks and operates purely as a syntax-translation engine, outputting object-oriented Python classes (\texttt{MPS}, \texttt{MPO}, and \texttt{DMRGEngine}).

At this juncture, the human researcher acts as the Principal Investigator. Rather than writing boilerplate code or hunting for missing syntax, the Human-in-the-Loop (HITL) executes the Jupyter Notebook and evaluates physical observables. If an error occurs—for instance, if a two-site DMRG update collapses to an unphysical bond dimension of $D=1$—the PI does not rewrite the code. Instead, the PI provides \textit{pedagogical feedback} to LLM-2 (Fig.~\ref{fig:workflow}D), explaining the physical impossibility of the result. Prompted with this physics-based insight, the LLM autonomously deduces the flaw in its contraction wiring and rewrites the function correctly.

\subsection{Accelerated Development Timeline}
The compartmentalization of tasks into this multi-agent framework yields a profound reduction in software development time. Writing a scalable, bug-free DMRG codebase from scratch traditionally requires months of specialized effort. 

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    % Ensure your image file is named appropriately, e.g., figure2.jpg
    \includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figure2.jpg} 
    \caption{\textbf{The Accelerated Development Timeline.} The complete workflow—from parsing the theoretical review paper to finalizing a verified, matrix-free DMRG codebase—was executed in under 24 hours of wall-clock time. This required approximately 14 hours of active, human-in-the-loop collaboration, representing a massive acceleration compared to traditional 3-to-6-month development cycles.}
    \label{fig:timeline}
\end{figure}

As mapped in Fig.~\ref{fig:timeline}, our complete workflow was systematically executed in less than 24 hours of wall-clock time. This comprised approximately 14 hours of active human-AI collaboration: 12 hours on Day 1 dedicated to theory extraction, expert blueprinting, and initial code generation, followed by an overnight break, and concluding with 2 hours of HITL physics verification on Day 2. By effectively flattening the learning and debugging curves, this workflow demonstrates how AI agents can rapidly accelerate the pace of quantum algorithm deployment.
% ---------------------------------------------------------
% End of Methodology Section
% ---------------------------------------------------------
```

### Why this section is incredibly strong:
1. **Flows logically with the images:** The text explicitly references the panels (A, B, C, D) in Figure 1, guiding the reader's eye to the exact visual proof of your claims.
2. **Elevates the Human Role:** The explanation of "Pedagogical Feedback" in Stage 3 proves that this isn't just a blind auto-coder. It shows a highly sophisticated, symbiotic relationship between the physicist and the AI.
3. **Validates the Timeline:** Figure 2 is heavily supported by the text. Breaking the 14 hours into "12 hours of setup" and "2 hours of verification" makes the efficiency claim highly believable and reproducible.

### LaTeX Source for the Physics Verification Section

Copy and paste this directly after your Reproducibility section.

```latex
% ---------------------------------------------------------
% Start of Physics Verification Section
% ---------------------------------------------------------
\section{Results: Physics Verification and Scalability}
\label{sec:physics_verification}

To definitively prove that the AI-generated codebase is both scalable and physically exact, we benchmarked the generated \texttt{DMRGEngine} against two paradigmatic 1D quantum many-body systems: the Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg model and the Spin-$1$ Affleck-Kennedy-Lieb-Tasaki (AKLT) model. The codebase successfully executed matrix-free Hamiltonian applications, completely avoiding the $\mathcal{O}(d^2 D^4)$ memory bottlenecks that typically plague naive tensor network implementations.

\subsection{Benchmark I: The Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg Chain}
We first tested the algorithm on the isotropic Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg chain with open boundary conditions (OBC) at system size $L=20$. Because the single-site DMRG algorithm is prone to becoming trapped in local energetic minima when initialized from a random state, the generated codebase successfully implemented a dynamic Two-Site DMRG warmup phase. 

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    % Ensure your Heisenberg plot is named correctly in your directory
    \includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{fig_heisenberg.pdf} 
    \caption{\textbf{Scalability and Convergence on the $L=20$ Heisenberg Model.} \textbf{(a)} Energy convergence demonstrating the hybrid workflow: a robust 2-site DMRG warmup efficiently expands the bond dimension (up to $D_{\max}=32$), followed by a rapid 1-site matrix-free refinement. \textbf{(b)} The accurately captured exponentially decaying connected spin-spin correlations $\langle S_i^z S_{i+r}^z \rangle_c$.}
    \label{fig:heisenberg}
\end{figure}

As shown in Fig.~\ref{fig:heisenberg}a, the two-site warmup efficiently expanded the bond dimension to $D_{\max}=32$, driving the system out of local minima and rapidly lowering the energy. This was followed by a highly optimized, matrix-free single-site refinement phase. The algorithm converged to the exact OBC ground-state energy ($E_0 \approx -8.682$). Furthermore, the code correctly evaluated the connected two-point correlation functions $\langle S_i^z S_{i+r}^z \rangle_c$, accurately capturing the expected spatial decay (Fig.~\ref{fig:heisenberg}b) without any explicit programming of the correlation network by the human PI.

\subsection{Benchmark II: Topological Order in the Spin-$1$ AKLT Model}
To test the engine's ability to capture complex non-local physics, we evaluated the Spin-$1$ AKLT model. The AKLT ground state is known to possess a gapped, symmetry-protected topological (SPT) phase, characterized by fractionalized edge spins and a hidden string order, which can be exactly represented by an MPS of bond dimension $D=2$.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    % Ensure your AKLT string order plot is named correctly
    \includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{fig_aklt.pdf} 
    \caption{\textbf{Verification of Topological Order in the AKLT Model.} \textbf{(a)} Rapid energy convergence to the exact analytical ground state $E_0 = -6.0$ for $L=10$. \textbf{(b)} The non-local string order parameter $\langle S_i^z \exp(i\pi \sum_{k=i+1}^{j-1} S_k^z) S_j^z \rangle$. The AI-generated code perfectly captures the hallmark hidden topological order of the Haldane phase, plateauing exactly at the theoretical value of $-4/9$ (dashed red line).}
    \label{fig:aklt}
\end{figure}

The generated code constructed the exact $D_W = 14$ Matrix Product Operator (MPO) for the AKLT biquadratic interaction. For a chain of $L=10$, the algorithm converged to the exact analytical energy $E_0 = -6.0$ up to machine precision (Fig.~\ref{fig:aklt}a). 

Crucially, to verify the physical fidelity of the generated tensor contractions, we prompted the implementation agent to calculate the non-local string order parameter:
\begin{equation}
    \mathcal{O}_{\text{string}}(i, j) = \langle S_i^z \exp\left(i\pi \sum_{k=i+1}^{j-1} S_k^z\right) S_j^z \rangle
\end{equation}
As demonstrated in Fig.~\ref{fig:aklt}b, the generated code flawlessly executed the multi-site string operator contraction. The correlator exhibits a perfectly flat plateau at exactly $-4/9$, the theoretical signature of the AKLT phase. This confirms that the multi-stage LLM workflow is capable of generating codebase architectures sophisticated enough to probe advanced topological properties in quantum many-body systems.
% ---------------------------------------------------------
% End of Physics Verification Section
% ---------------------------------------------------------
```

### Updated LaTeX Source for Physics Verification

```latex
% ---------------------------------------------------------
% Start of Physics Verification Section
% ---------------------------------------------------------
\section{Results: Physics Verification and Scalability}
\label{sec:physics_verification}

To definitively prove that the AI-generated codebase is both scalable and physically exact, we benchmarked the \texttt{DMRGEngine} against two paradigmatic 1D quantum many-body systems: the Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg model and the Spin-$1$ Affleck-Kennedy-Lieb-Tasaki (AKLT) model. Crucially, the codebase successfully executed matrix-free Hamiltonian applications in both cases, completely avoiding the $\mathcal{O}(d^2 D^4)$ memory bottlenecks that typically plague naive tensor network implementations.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    % Ensure your combined plot is named appropriately, e.g., fig_physics.pdf
    % The figure should contain 4 panels: 
    % (a) Heisenberg Energy, (b) Heisenberg Correlations, 
    % (c) AKLT Energy, (d) AKLT String Order
    \includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{fig_physics.pdf} 
    \caption{\textbf{Physics Verification and Scalability Benchmarks.} The AI-generated codebase accurately captures the distinct physics of both critical and gapped topological phases. \textbf{(a)} Energy convergence for the $L=20$ Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg chain, demonstrating a robust 2-site DMRG warmup that efficiently expands the bond dimension (up to $D_{\max}=32$), followed by a rapid 1-site matrix-free refinement. \textbf{(b)} The corresponding exponentially decaying connected spin-spin correlations $\langle S_i^z S_{i+r}^z \rangle_c$. \textbf{(c)} Rapid energy convergence of the Spin-$1$ AKLT model to the exact analytical ground state $E_0 = -6.0$ for $L=10$, utilizing the exact $D_W=14$ MPO. \textbf{(d)} The non-local string order parameter $\langle S_i^z \exp(i\pi \sum_{k=i+1}^{j-1} S_k^z) S_j^z \rangle$ for the AKLT model, perfectly plateauing at the theoretical value of $-4/9$ (dashed red line), confirming the code captures the hidden topological order of the Haldane phase.}
    \label{fig:physics}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Benchmark I: The Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg Chain}
We first tested the algorithm on the isotropic Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg chain with open boundary conditions (OBC) at system size $L=20$. Because the single-site DMRG algorithm is prone to becoming trapped in local energetic minima when initialized from a random state, the generated codebase successfully implemented a dynamic Two-Site DMRG warmup phase. 

As shown in Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}a, the two-site warmup efficiently expanded the bond dimension to $D_{\max}=32$, driving the system out of local minima and rapidly lowering the energy. This was followed by a highly optimized, matrix-free single-site refinement phase. The algorithm converged to the exact OBC ground-state energy ($E_0 \approx -8.682$). Furthermore, the code correctly evaluated the connected two-point correlation functions $\langle S_i^z S_{i+r}^z \rangle_c$, accurately capturing the expected spatial decay (Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}b) without any explicit programming of the correlation network by the human PI.

\subsection{Benchmark II: Topological Order in the Spin-$1$ AKLT Model}
To test the engine's ability to capture complex non-local physics, we evaluated the Spin-$1$ AKLT model. The AKLT ground state is known to possess a gapped, symmetry-protected topological (SPT) phase, characterized by fractionalized edge spins and a hidden string order, which can be exactly represented by an MPS of bond dimension $D=2$.

The generated code constructed the exact $D_W = 14$ Matrix Product Operator (MPO) for the AKLT biquadratic interaction. For a chain of $L=10$, the algorithm converged to the exact analytical energy $E_0 = -6.0$ up to machine precision (Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}c). 

Crucially, to verify the physical fidelity of the generated tensor contractions, we prompted the implementation agent to calculate the non-local string order parameter:
\begin{equation}
    \mathcal{O}_{\text{string}}(i, j) = \langle S_i^z \exp\left(i\pi \sum_{k=i+1}^{j-1} S_k^z\right) S_j^z \rangle
\end{equation}
As demonstrated in Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}d, the generated code flawlessly executed the multi-site string operator contraction. The correlator exhibits a perfectly flat plateau at exactly $-4/9$, the theoretical signature of the AKLT phase. This confirms that the multi-stage LLM workflow is capable of generating codebase architectures sophisticated enough to probe advanced topological properties in quantum many-body systems.
% ---------------------------------------------------------
% End of Physics Verification Section
% ---------------------------------------------------------
```

### Tips for creating `fig_physics.pdf`
When you combine your plots into a single figure file (using Python, Illustrator, Inkscape, or Keynote), arrange them in a $2 \times 2$ grid:
*   **Top Left (a):** Heisenberg Energy Convergence
*   **Top Right (b):** Heisenberg Connected Correlations
*   **Bottom Left (c):** AKLT Energy Convergence
*   **Bottom Right (d):** AKLT String Order Plateau

Make sure the font sizes on the axes (labels, ticks, and legends) are large enough to be easily readable when the 4-panel image is scaled down to fit on a standard A4/Letter journal page.

### Updated LaTeX Source for Physics Verification

```latex
% ---------------------------------------------------------
% Start of Physics Verification Section
% ---------------------------------------------------------
\section{Results: Physics Verification and Scalability}
\label{sec:physics_verification}

To definitively prove that the AI-generated codebase is both scalable and physically exact, we benchmarked the \texttt{DMRGEngine} against two paradigmatic 1D quantum many-body systems: the Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg model (a critical phase) and the Spin-$1$ Affleck-Kennedy-Lieb-Tasaki (AKLT) model (a gapped topological phase). The codebase successfully executed matrix-free Hamiltonian applications in both cases, completely avoiding the $\mathcal{O}(d^2 D^4)$ memory bottlenecks that typically plague naive tensor network implementations.

\begin{figure}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.98\textwidth]{fig_physics.png} 
    \caption{\textbf{Physics Verification and Scaling Benchmarks.} The AI-generated codebase accurately captures the distinct physics of critical and gapped topological phases. \textbf{(a)} Ground state energy $E_0$ of the $L=12$ Heisenberg chain extrapolated against the inverse bond dimension $1/D$. \textbf{(b)} Finite-size scaling of the Heisenberg ground state energy density $E_0/L$ versus $1/L$, accurately extrapolating to the exact Bethe Ansatz thermodynamic limit ($e_\infty = -0.4431$). \textbf{(c)} Bipartite entanglement entropy profile for the $L=12$ Heisenberg chain, exhibiting expected even-odd boundary oscillations and matching the Conformal Field Theory (CFT) prediction for central charge $c=1$. \textbf{(d)} Ground state energy of the AKLT model perfectly matching the exact analytical formula $E_0 = -(L-1)\cdot 2/3$ across various system sizes. \textbf{(e)} Bond entanglement entropy for the AKLT model ($D=2$), showing the bulk bonds plateauing exactly at $\ln 2 \approx 0.6931$, reflecting the fractionalized virtual spin-$1/2$ singlets of the valence-bond solid state. \textbf{(f)} The non-local string order parameter perfectly plateauing at the theoretical value of $-4/9$, confirming the hidden topological order of the Haldane phase.}
    \label{fig:physics}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Benchmark I: Criticality in the Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg Chain}
We first tested the algorithm on the isotropic Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg chain with open boundary conditions (OBC). Critical 1D systems are notoriously difficult for MPS algorithms due to the logarithmic divergence of entanglement entropy, making them ideal stress tests for the generated code. 

As shown in Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}a, the generated code successfully implements bond-dimension scaling. For a system of size $L=12$, the ground-state energy $E_0$ converges smoothly as a function of the inverse bond dimension $1/D$. To verify the code's accuracy in the thermodynamic limit, we performed a finite-size scaling (FSS) analysis (Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}b). By plotting the energy density $E_0/L$ against $1/L$, we extrapolated the bulk energy density to $e_\infty = -0.4427$, in excellent agreement with the exact Bethe Ansatz value of $-0.4431$.

Furthermore, the code was utilized to extract the bipartite entanglement entropy $S$ across the chain (Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}c). The generated engine accurately resolved both the prominent even-odd Friedel oscillations induced by the open boundaries and the overarching logarithmic scaling profile predicted by Conformal Field Theory (CFT) for a Tomonaga-Luttinger liquid with central charge $c=1$.

\subsection{Benchmark II: Topological Order in the Spin-$1$ AKLT Model}
To test the engine's ability to capture complex non-local physics and gapped phases, we evaluated the Spin-$1$ AKLT model. The AKLT ground state is a symmetry-protected topological (SPT) phase, characterized by fractionalized edge spins and a hidden string order, which can be exactly represented by an MPS of bond dimension $D=2$.

The generated code successfully constructed the exact $D_W = 14$ Matrix Product Operator (MPO) for the AKLT biquadratic interaction. As demonstrated in Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}d, the DMRG-computed ground state energies precisely matched the analytical formula $E_0 = -(L-1) \cdot 2/3$ across multiple system sizes. 

The physical fidelity of the tensor network is further proven by the entanglement spectrum (Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}e). The code accurately resolved the bulk bond entanglement entropy plateauing at exactly $\ln 2 \approx 0.6931$. This perfectly reflects the underlying valence-bond solid (VBS) picture, where cutting any bulk bond severs exactly one maximally entangled virtual spin-$1/2$ singlet.

Finally, we prompted the implementation agent to calculate the non-local string order parameter:
\begin{equation}
    \mathcal{O}_{\text{string}}(i_0, i_0+r) = \langle S_{i_0}^z \exp\left(i\pi \sum_{k=i_0+1}^{i_0+r-1} S_k^z\right) S_{i_0+r}^z \rangle
\end{equation}
As shown in Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}f, the generated code flawlessly executed this multi-site string operator contraction. The correlator exhibits a perfectly flat plateau at exactly $-4/9$, the theoretical signature of the AKLT phase. This confirms that the multi-stage LLM workflow is capable of generating codebase architectures sophisticated enough to probe both critical entanglement scaling and advanced topological properties in quantum many-body systems.
% ---------------------------------------------------------
% End of Physics Verification Section
% ---------------------------------------------------------
```

*   **"$\ln 2$ Plateau":** Explicitly connecting the $\ln 2$ entropy to the cutting of a virtual spin-1/2 singlet proves that the code isn't just crunching numbers; it captures the exact topological VBS state correctly.

Here is the complete LaTeX code for the top half of your paper, including the **Title, Author Affiliations, Abstract, and the Introduction**. 

This Introduction perfectly sets the stage for your paper. ### LaTeX Source: Top Half of the Paper (Title $\to$ Introduction)

```latex
\documentclass[aps,prb,onecolumn,scriptaddress]{revtex4-2}
\usepackage{graphicx,amsmath,amsfonts,amssymb,bm,algorithm,algpseudocode,booktabs}
\usepackage[colorlinks=true, urlcolor=blue, linkcolor=blue, citecolor=blue]{hyperref}
\usepackage{tabularx}

\begin{document}

\title{From Paper to Program: A Multi-Stage LLM-Assisted Workflow \\ 
for Accelerating Quantum Many-Body Algorithm Development}

\author{Yi Zhou}
\email{yizhou@iphy.ac.cn}
\affiliation{Institute of Physics, Chinese Academy of Sciences, Beijing 100190, China}

\date{\today}

\begin{abstract}
Translating abstract quantum many-body theory into scalable, bug-free software represents a major bottleneck in computational physics, traditionally requiring months of specialized human effort. While Large Language Models (LLMs) excel at standard programming tasks, zero-shot attempts to generate complex tensor network algorithms frequently fail due to spatial reasoning errors, index mismatches, and severe memory inefficiencies. To overcome this, we introduce a multi-stage, human-in-the-loop (HITL) workflow that mimics the pedagogical structure of a theoretical physics research group. Rather than generating code directly from source literature, our workflow introduces a critical intermediate step: an ``expert reviewer'' model (LLM-1) processes the initial theory extraction (LLM-0) to generate a mathematically rigorous, highly optimized LaTeX specification. This blueprint enforces universal index conventions and explicitly outlines matrix-free tensor contractions, effectively constraining the final ``coder'' model (LLM-2). We validate this methodology using the Density-Matrix Renormalization Group (DMRG) algorithm. The generated codebase successfully implements scalable, $\mathcal{O}(D^3)$ matrix-free solvers, accurately reproducing the exact ground-state energies and Conformal Field Theory (CFT) entropy profiles of the Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg chain, as well as the non-local topological string order of the Spin-$1$ AKLT model. To demonstrate universal reproducibility, we tested a $4 \times 4$ grid of state-of-the-art foundation models (GPT, Claude, Gemini, and Kimi/Kimi Agent) for the LLM-1 and LLM-2 stages, achieving a 100\% (16/16) success rate. Remarkably, the end-to-end development of each verified physics engine required less than 24 hours of wall-clock time, comprising approximately 14 hours of active human-AI collaboration. This framework provides a model-agnostic, highly reproducible blueprint for radically accelerating software development across the physical sciences.
\end{abstract}

\maketitle

% ---------------------------------------------------------
% Start of Introduction Section
% ---------------------------------------------------------
\section{Introduction}
\label{sec:intro}

The numerical simulation of strongly correlated quantum many-body systems has been fundamentally transformed by the advent of tensor network methods. In one spatial dimension, the Density-Matrix Renormalization Group (DMRG) algorithm \cite{White1992, Schollwock2011} and the associated Matrix Product State (MPS) formalism provide an essentially exact description of both critical and gapped quantum phases. However, translating the continuous, analytical mathematics of tensor network theory into discrete, high-performance software remains a formidable challenge. 

Developing a production-ready DMRG codebase from scratch traditionally requires months of dedicated graduate-level effort. The difficulty lies not in the conceptual physics, but in the stringent demands of computational implementation: researchers must meticulously track multi-dimensional array indices during tensor contractions (e.g., via \texttt{numpy.einsum}), manage gauge degrees of freedom to maintain canonical forms, and bypass $\mathcal{O}(D^4)$ memory blowups by designing matrix-free iterative eigensolvers (where $D$ is the virtual bond dimension). Consequently, there is a steep learning curve that bottlenecks the rapid prototyping of new algorithms and tensor geometries.

Simultaneously, the rapid advancement of Large Language Models (LLMs) has revolutionized general-purpose software engineering. Foundation models can now generate, debug, and refactor code for web development and data science with remarkable proficiency. Yet, when applied to computational physics, these models frequently stumble. Zero-shot attempts to generate tensor network algorithms directly from theoretical literature typically result in ``hallucinatory'' code. Because LLMs lack intrinsic spatial reasoning, they frequently output mismatched tensor legs, fail to distinguish between complex conjugation and conjugate transposition, or propose naive dense-matrix contractions that instantly exhaust system memory. Thus, a direct ``Paper to Program'' translation using isolated AI prompts is currently unviable for advanced scientific computing.

In this work, we propose a solution to this bottleneck: a multi-stage, Human-in-the-Loop (HITL) workflow that conceptualizes the AI agents not as monolithic code-generators, but as a ``Virtual Research Group.'' By dividing the development process into specialized roles—a Junior Theorist (LLM-0) for literature extraction, a Senior Postdoc (LLM-1) for formal specification, and a Research Assistant (LLM-2) for code generation—we demonstrate that the reliability of AI-assisted programming can be drastically improved. 

The core innovation of our methodology is the introduction of the \textit{Intermediate Technical Specification}. We demonstrate that by forcing the workflow to pause and generate a mathematically rigorous LaTeX blueprint (enforcing universal index conventions and matrix-free logic), the variance and hallucination rates of the coding LLM are effectively eliminated. The mathematical LaTeX acts as a ``Universal API,'' transforming a highly complex physics-reasoning task into a strictly constrained syntax-translation task.

To benchmark this workflow, we generated a complete, object-oriented MPS and DMRG engine in Python. We demonstrate its physical exactness by successfully resolving the critical scaling of the Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg model and the hidden topological order of the Spin-$1$ Affleck-Kennedy-Lieb-Tasaki (AKLT) model. Furthermore, we rigorously prove the reproducibility of this method by testing it across a $4 \times 4$ grid of modern foundation models (Kimi, Gemini, GPT, and Claude), achieving a 100\% (16 out of 16) success rate. Most significantly, this multi-agent workflow compressed a traditional months-long development cycle into less than 24 hours of wall-clock time ($\sim 14$ active hours), establishing a highly reproducible paradigm for accelerating scientific software development.

The remainder of this paper is organized as follows. In Sec.~\ref{sec:methodology}, we detail the architecture of the ``Virtual Research Group'' workflow and the specific roles of each LLM agent. In Sec.~\ref{sec:reproducibility}, we present the results of our cross-model reproducibility matrix and analyze the accelerated development timeline. In Sec.~\ref{sec:physics_verification}, we provide the physics verification benchmarks for the generated codebase on the Heisenberg and AKLT models. Finally, we conclude in Sec.~\ref{sec:discussion} with a discussion on the implications of structured LLM specifications for the future of computational physics.

% ---------------------------------------------------------
% End of Introduction Section
% ---------------------------------------------------------
```

### Why this Introduction is highly effective:
1. **The Hook:** It identifies a real, painful problem that every computational physicist knows well: debugging tensor indices and memory issues takes *months*.
2. **The Gap in Current AI:** It acknowledges that standard ChatGPT is terrible at writing physics codes directly (zero-shot), building credibility that you aren't just over-hyping AI.
3. **The Innovation:** It clearly defines the "Intermediate Technical Specification" (the LaTeX document) as the central contribution of the paper.
4. **The Punchline:** It ends the third paragraph by dropping the massive metrics: **16/16 success rate** and **<24 hour turnaround time**.

You are basically finished! All that remains is a brief **Conclusion/Discussion** section to wrap it up, and assembling the bibliography. Would you like me to draft the Conclusion next?

### LaTeX Source: Discussion & Conclusion

```latex
% ---------------------------------------------------------
% Start of Discussion and Conclusion Section
% ---------------------------------------------------------
\section{Discussion and Conclusion}
\label{sec:discussion}

The integration of Large Language Models into scientific computing promises to radically accelerate the pace of research, yet zero-shot code generation consistently fails when applied to advanced quantum many-body algorithms. In this work, we demonstrated that the bottleneck is not an inherent limitation in the reasoning capabilities of modern foundation models, but rather a failure of workflow architecture. By structuring the AI agents as a ``Virtual Research Group''—separating the tasks of theory extraction, rigorous mathematical specification, and syntax implementation—we successfully bridged the gap between abstract theoretical literature and scalable, production-ready software.

The central innovation of this methodology is the introduction of the intermediate LaTeX specification (LLM-1). We found that providing a coding agent (LLM-2) with a raw, unoptimized extraction of physical equations inevitably leads to fatal spatial reasoning errors, such as hallucinated tensor contractions and unmitigated $\mathcal{O}(D^6)$ memory blowups. However, by forcing an ``expert reviewer'' agent to first generate a mathematically airtight LaTeX blueprint—explicitly enforcing universal index conventions and matrix-free $\mathcal{O}(D^3)$ tensor limits—the final coding task is reduced to a highly constrained syntax translation. The mathematical LaTeX acts as a ``Universal API,'' flawlessly coordinating different AI ecosystems (e.g., OpenAI's GPT, Google's Gemini, Anthropic's Claude, and Moonshot's Kimi/Kimi Agent) and yielding a 100\% (16/16) cross-model reproducibility rate.

Furthermore, this workflow redefines the role of the computational physicist. The Human-in-the-Loop (HITL) is elevated from writing boilerplate syntax to acting as a Principal Investigator (PI). During our 14 hours of active collaboration, human intervention was reserved exclusively for high-level physics verification—such as providing pedagogical feedback when the two-site DMRG algorithm unphysically collapsed to a bond dimension of $D=1$. Instructed purely on the physical logic, the AI autonomously diagnosed its contraction wiring error and implemented the correct algorithmic structure. 

The physical exactness of the generated codebase was rigorously validated against paradigmatic 1D quantum systems. The engine accurately captured the Conformal Field Theory (CFT) logarithmic entanglement scaling of the critical Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg chain, and precisely reproduced the fractionalized edge states and $-4/9$ non-local string order plateau of the gapped Spin-$1$ AKLT model. 

Most remarkably, this entire development cycle—from reading the foundational review paper to deploying a verified, scalable codebase—was completed in under 24 hours of wall-clock time. This represents a compression of the traditional software development timeline by several orders of magnitude. 

Looking forward, this multi-stage, specification-driven workflow is highly generalizable. We anticipate that this paradigm can be immediately applied to accelerate the development of even more complex quantum algorithms, such as Time-Dependent Variational Principle (TDVP) engines, infinite-system DMRG (iDMRG), and 2D tensor network frameworks like Projected Entangled Pair States (PEPS). By effectively eliminating the coding and debugging bottleneck, this approach frees researchers to focus entirely on algorithmic design and physical discovery.

\begin{acknowledgments}
We acknowledge the use of Kimi (Moonshot AI), Gemini (Google), GPT (OpenAI), and Claude (Anthropic) in the execution of the multi-agent workflow. [Add any funding agencies or grants here]. 
\end{acknowledgments}

% Bibliography
% \bibliographystyle{apsrev4-2}
% \bibliography{references} % Create a references.bib file for your citations

\end{document}
```

Switching to a `twocolumn` format is the perfect move. It will immediately make your manuscript look like a professional, submission-ready APS journal article (like *Physical Review B* or *PRL*).

When moving to a two-column layout, the most important thing to know is how to handle your figures. Because your figures (especially the 6-panel physics plot and the Nano Banana workflow diagrams) are highly detailed, **they need to span across both columns** so they don't get squished. In LaTeX, you do this by changing `\begin{figure}` to `\begin{figure*}` (adding the asterisk).

```latex
\section{Introduction}
\label{sec:intro}
% =========================================================

The numerical simulation of strongly correlated quantum many-body systems has been fundamentally transformed by the advent of tensor network methods. % =========================================================
\section{Methodology: The ``Virtual Research Group'' Workflow}
\label{sec:methodology}
% =========================================================

Translating continuous quantum many-body theory into discrete, high-performance array operations requires tacit computational knowledge that is rarely explicit in physical literature. To navigate this, we structured our multi-agent LLM pipeline to mimic the pedagogical and hierarchical dynamics of a traditional academic research group. As illustrated in Fig.~\ref{fig:workflow}, the workflow partitions algorithm development into three distinct LLM roles, supervised by a human Principal Investigator (PI).

% NOTE: Using figure* to span both columns for the large workflow diagram
\begin{figure*}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth]{figure1.jpg} 
    \caption{\textbf{The ``Paper to Program'' Multi-Agent Workflow.} The development process mimics a virtual research group. \textbf{(A)} The theoretical source material. \textbf{(B)} A zero-shot translation by LLM-0 (``Junior Theorist'') yields a flawed initial draft, characterized by hallucinated tensor indices and severe memory scaling bottlenecks. \textbf{(C)} The crucial intermediate step: LLM-1 (``Senior Postdoc'') reviews and corrects the draft, generating a mathematically rigorous formal specification. This stage enforces universal index conventions and explicit memory optimizations (e.g., \texttt{optimize=True}). \textbf{(D)} Constrained by this strict blueprint, LLM-2 (``Coder'') reliably generates scalable Python code. The Human PI remains in the loop strictly for high-level physics verification and pedagogical feedback.}
    \label{fig:workflow}
\end{figure*}

\subsection{Stage 1: Theory Extraction (LLM-0 / The ``Junior Theorist'')}
The workflow begins by providing the source literature—in this case, the comprehensive review of MPS and DMRG by Schollw\"ock (2011)—to the first foundation model, LLM-0 (Kimi 2.5). The objective of this agent is to extract the fundamental theoretical equations, such as Matrix Product Operator (MPO) representations, QR/SVD canonicalization routines, and effective Hamiltonian contractions, and translate them into an initial LaTeX draft.

Much like a junior graduate student, LLM-0 succeeds at extracting the broad mathematical strokes but frequently fails to account for computational realities. As depicted in Fig.~\ref{fig:workflow}B, direct extraction often results in messy pseudo-code containing hallucinatory index mappings, missing library prefixes, and a failure to optimize tensor contraction paths, which would immediately lead to fatal $\mathcal{O}(D^6)$ memory blowups if executed.

\subsection{Stage 2: Expert Specification (LLM-1 / The ``Senior Postdoc'')}
The most critical innovation of this workflow is Stage 2. We expressly forbid the direct translation of the Stage 1 draft into Python. Instead, the draft is passed to an ``expert reviewer'' model, LLM-1 (Gemini 3.1), tasked with line-by-line verification for computational and coding readiness. 

LLM-1 acts as the senior specifying agent. It autonomously identifies algorithmic ambiguities and injects tacit physics domain knowledge to create a rigorous formal blueprint (Fig.~\ref{fig:workflow}C). Key optimizations introduced at this stage include:
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Universal Index Conventions:} Explicitly defining a rigid nomenclature for tensor legs (e.g., \texttt{b/B} for MPO bonds, \texttt{x/X} for bra bonds, \texttt{y/Y} for ket bonds) to entirely eliminate broadcasting errors.
    \item \textbf{Matrix-Free Scalability:} Replacing dense matrix constructions with iterative \texttt{scipy.sparse.linalg.LinearOperator} implementations, ensuring the effective Hamiltonian application scales strictly as $\mathcal{O}(D^3)$.
    \item \textbf{Memory Management:} Enforcing the use of \texttt{np.tensordot} for gauge shifting to leverage optimized BLAS routines, and differentiating between memory views and deep copies in NumPy array reshaping.
\end{itemize}
The output of this stage is a flawlessly compiled, highly detailed LaTeX document. This document serves as a universal, model-agnostic Application Programming Interface (API) for the final coding stage.

\subsection{Stage 3: Code Implementation \& HITL Mentorship (LLM-2 \& Human PI)}
In the final stage, the formal LaTeX specification is passed to the implementation agent, LLM-2. Because the LaTeX blueprint mathematically constrains the tensor shapes and contraction paths, LLM-2 is relieved of spatial reasoning tasks and operates purely as a syntax-translation engine, outputting object-oriented Python classes.

At this juncture, the human researcher acts as the Principal Investigator. Rather than writing boilerplate code or hunting for missing syntax, the Human-in-the-Loop (HITL) executes the Jupyter Notebook and evaluates physical observables. If an error occurs—for instance, if a two-site DMRG update collapses to an unphysical bond dimension of $D=1$—the PI does not rewrite the code. Instead, the PI provides \textit{pedagogical feedback} to LLM-2 (Fig.~\ref{fig:workflow}D), explaining the physical impossibility of the result. Prompted with this physics-based insight, the LLM autonomously deduces the flaw in its contraction wiring and rewrites the function correctly.

% NOTE: Using figure* for the timeline so it spans two columns
\begin{figure*}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth]{figure2.jpg} 
    \caption{\textbf{The Accelerated Development Timeline.} The complete workflow—from parsing the theoretical review paper to finalizing a verified, matrix-free DMRG codebase—was executed in under 24 hours of wall-clock time. This required approximately 14 hours of active, human-in-the-loop collaboration, representing a massive acceleration compared to traditional 3-to-6-month development cycles.}
    \label{fig:timeline}
\end{figure*}

% =========================================================
\section{Results: Systematic Reproducibility and Workflow Acceleration}
\label{sec:reproducibility}
% =========================================================

To rigorously evaluate the robustness of our multi-stage workflow, we conducted a systematic cross-compatibility test using a diverse set of state-of-the-art foundation models: Kimi, Gemini, GPT, and Claude. The initial theoretical extraction (LLM-0) was kept constant, while the models utilized for the expert specification (LLM-1) and the code implementation (LLM-2) were permuted to form a $4 \times 4$ testing grid.

As shown in Table~\ref{tab:reproducibility}, we achieved a 100\% success rate (16 out of 16 paths). A path was deemed successful if the final generated Python codebase executed without shape-mismatch or memory-allocation errors, successfully implemented the $\mathcal{O}(D^3)$ matrix-free Lanczos solver, and accurately reproduced the exact ground-state energy and topological string order of the benchmark models.

% NOTE: The table remains single-column as it is small enough to fit.
\begin{table}[htbp]
    \centering
    \caption{\textbf{Cross-model reproducibility matrix.} All 16 combinations of LLM-1 (Specification) and LLM-2 (Code Implementation) successfully produced a scalable DMRG codebase. A checkmark ($\checkmark$) indicates successful physics verification. Notably, when Kimi was utilized for the code generation stage, the autonomous `Kimi Agent' framework was deployed.}
    \label{tab:reproducibility}
    \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}
    \begin{tabular}{@{\extracolsep{6pt}}lcccc@{}}
        \toprule
        & \multicolumn{4}{c}{\textbf{LLM-2 (Code Implementation)}} \\
        \cmidrule{2-5}
        \textbf{LLM-1 (Specifier)} & Kimi Agent & Gemini & GPT & Claude \\
        \midrule
        \textbf{Kimi}   & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ \\
        \textbf{Gemini} & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ \\
        \textbf{GPT}    & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ \\
        \textbf{Claude} & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ \\
        \bottomrule
    \end{tabular}
\end{table}

This perfect completion rate underscores a fundamental insight: the primary cause of failure in zero-shot code generation is not an inherent limitation in the reasoning capabilities of the models, but rather the absence of strict computational definitions. The rigorous LaTeX specification acts as a ``Universal API''—a model-agnostic blueprint that completely flattens the performance variance between different AI ecosystems (e.g., seamlessly bridging OpenAI's GPT with Moonshot AI's Kimi Agent).

Furthermore, despite the cross-model handoffs, the total development time for each of the 16 successful paths remained under 24 hours of wall-clock time (Fig.~\ref{fig:timeline}). On average, this corresponded to approximately 14 hours of active, human-in-the-loop collaboration—a reduction of several orders of magnitude compared to traditional tensor network software development.

% =========================================================
\section{Results: Physics Verification and Scalability}
\label{sec:physics_verification}
% =========================================================

To definitively prove that the AI-generated codebase is both scalable and physically exact, we benchmarked the \texttt{DMRGEngine} against two paradigmatic 1D quantum many-body systems: the Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg model (a critical phase) and the Spin-$1$ AKLT model (a gapped topological phase). The codebase successfully executed matrix-free Hamiltonian applications in both cases, completely avoiding the $\mathcal{O}(d^2 D^4)$ memory bottlenecks.

% NOTE: Using figure* for the 6-panel physics plot so it spans both columns
\begin{figure*}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.98\textwidth]{fig_physics.png} 
    \caption{\textbf{Physics Verification and Scaling Benchmarks.} The AI-generated codebase accurately captures the distinct physics of critical and gapped topological phases. \textbf{(a)} Ground state energy $E_0$ of the $L=12$ Heisenberg chain extrapolated against the inverse bond dimension $1/D$. \textbf{(b)} Finite-size scaling of the Heisenberg ground state energy density $E_0/L$ versus $1/L$, accurately extrapolating to the exact Bethe Ansatz thermodynamic limit ($e_\infty = -0.4431$). \textbf{(c)} Bipartite entanglement entropy profile for the $L=12$ Heisenberg chain, matching the Conformal Field Theory (CFT) prediction for central charge $c=1$. \textbf{(d)} Ground state energy of the AKLT model perfectly matching the exact analytical formula across various system sizes. \textbf{(e)} Bond entanglement entropy for the AKLT model ($D=2$), showing the bulk bonds plateauing exactly at $\ln 2 \approx 0.6931$. \textbf{(f)} The non-local string order parameter perfectly plateauing at the theoretical value of $-4/9$, confirming the hidden topological order of the Haldane phase.}
    \label{fig:physics}
\end{figure*}

\subsection{Benchmark I: Criticality in the Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg Chain}
We first tested the algorithm on the isotropic Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg chain with open boundary conditions (OBC). Critical 1D systems are notoriously difficult for MPS algorithms due to the logarithmic divergence of entanglement entropy, making them ideal stress tests for the generated code. 

As shown in Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}a, the generated code successfully implements bond-dimension scaling. For a system of size $L=12$, the ground-state energy $E_0$ converges smoothly as a function of the inverse bond dimension $1/D$. To verify the code's accuracy in the thermodynamic limit, we performed a finite-size scaling (FSS) analysis (Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}b). By plotting the energy density $E_0/L$ against $1/L$, we extrapolated the bulk energy density to $e_\infty = -0.4427$, in excellent agreement with the exact Bethe Ansatz value of $-0.4431$.

Furthermore, the code was utilized to extract the bipartite entanglement entropy $S$ across the chain (Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}c). The generated engine accurately resolved both the prominent even-odd Friedel oscillations induced by the open boundaries and the overarching logarithmic scaling profile predicted by Conformal Field Theory (CFT) for a Tomonaga-Luttinger liquid with central charge $c=1$.

\subsection{Benchmark II: Topological Order in the Spin-$1$ AKLT Model}
To test the engine's ability to capture complex non-local physics and gapped phases, we evaluated the Spin-$1$ AKLT model. The AKLT ground state is a symmetry-protected topological (SPT) phase, characterized by fractionalized edge spins and a hidden string order, which can be exactly represented by an MPS of bond dimension $D=2$.

The generated code successfully constructed the exact $D_W = 14$ Matrix Product Operator (MPO) for the AKLT biquadratic interaction. As demonstrated in Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}d, the DMRG-computed ground state energies precisely matched the analytical formula $E_0 = -(L-1) \cdot 2/3$ across multiple system sizes. The physical fidelity of the tensor network is further proven by the entanglement spectrum (Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}e). The code accurately resolved the bulk bond entanglement entropy plateauing at exactly $\ln 2 \approx 0.6931$. This perfectly reflects the underlying valence-bond solid (VBS) picture, where cutting any bulk bond severs exactly one maximally entangled virtual spin-$1/2$ singlet.

Finally, we prompted the implementation agent to calculate the non-local string order parameter:
\begin{equation}
    \mathcal{O}_{\text{string}}(i_0, i_0+r) = \langle S_{i_0}^z \exp\left(i\pi \sum_{k=i_0+1}^{i_0+r-1} S_k^z\right) S_{i_0+r}^z \rangle
\end{equation}
As shown in Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}f, the generated code flawlessly executed this multi-site string operator contraction. The correlator exhibits a perfectly flat plateau at exactly $-4/9$, the theoretical signature of the AKLT phase. 

% =========================================================
\section{Discussion and Conclusion}
\label{sec:discussion}
% =========================================================

The integration of Large Language Models into scientific computing promises to radically accelerate the pace of research, yet zero-shot code generation consistently fails when applied to advanced quantum many-body algorithms. In this work, we demonstrated that the bottleneck is not an inherent limitation in the reasoning capabilities of modern foundation models, but rather a failure of workflow architecture. By structuring the AI agents as a ``Virtual Research Group''—separating the tasks of theory extraction, rigorous mathematical specification, and syntax implementation—we successfully bridged the gap between abstract theoretical literature and scalable, production-ready software.

The central innovation of this methodology is the introduction of the intermediate LaTeX specification (LLM-1). We found that providing a coding agent (LLM-2) with a raw, unoptimized extraction of physical equations inevitably leads to fatal spatial reasoning errors, such as hallucinated tensor contractions and unmitigated $\mathcal{O}(D^6)$ memory blowups. However, by forcing an ``expert reviewer'' agent to first generate a mathematically airtight LaTeX blueprint, the final coding task is reduced to a highly constrained syntax translation. The mathematical LaTeX acts as a ``Universal API,'' flawlessly coordinating different AI ecosystems and yielding a 100\% (16/16) cross-model reproducibility rate.

Furthermore, this workflow redefines the role of the computational physicist. The Human-in-the-Loop is elevated from writing boilerplate syntax to acting as a Principal Investigator. Instructed purely on physical logic (e.g., correcting unphysical state collapses), the AI autonomously diagnosed its contraction wiring errors and implemented the correct algorithmic structure. Most remarkably, this entire development cycle was completed in under 24 hours of wall-clock time, compressing the traditional software development timeline by several orders of magnitude. 

Looking forward, this multi-stage, specification-driven workflow is highly generalizable. We anticipate that this paradigm can be immediately applied to accelerate the development of even more complex quantum algorithms, such as Time-Dependent Variational Principle (TDVP) engines, infinite-system DMRG (iDMRG), and 2D tensor network frameworks like Projected Entangled Pair States (PEPS). By effectively eliminating the coding and debugging bottleneck, this approach frees researchers to focus entirely on algorithmic design and physical discovery.

% =========================================================

\end{acknowledgments}
```

```latex
\documentclass[aps,prb,twocolumn,superscriptaddress]{revtex4-2}
\usepackage{graphicx,amsmath,amsfonts,amssymb,bm,algorithm,algpseudocode,booktabs}
\usepackage[colorlinks=true, urlcolor=blue, linkcolor=blue, citecolor=blue]{hyperref}

\begin{document}

\title{From Paper to Program: A Multi-Stage LLM-Assisted Workflow \\ 
for Accelerating Quantum Many-Body Algorithm Development}

\author{Yi Zhou}
\email{yizhou@iphy.ac.cn}
\affiliation{Institute of Physics, Chinese Academy of Sciences, Beijing 100190, China}

\date{\today}

\begin{abstract}
Translating abstract quantum many-body theory into scalable, bug-free software represents a major bottleneck in computational physics, traditionally requiring months of specialized human effort. While Large Language Models (LLMs) excel at standard programming tasks, zero-shot attempts to generate complex tensor network algorithms frequently fail due to spatial reasoning errors, index mismatches, and severe memory inefficiencies. To overcome this, we introduce a multi-stage, human-in-the-loop (HITL) workflow that mimics the pedagogical structure of a theoretical physics research group. Rather than generating code directly from source literature, our workflow introduces a critical intermediate step: an ``expert reviewer'' model (LLM-1) processes the initial theory extraction (LLM-0) to generate a mathematically rigorous, highly optimized LaTeX specification. This blueprint enforces universal index conventions and explicitly outlines matrix-free tensor contractions, effectively constraining the final ``coder'' model (LLM-2). We validate this methodology using the Density-Matrix Renormalization Group (DMRG) algorithm. The generated codebase successfully implements scalable, $\mathcal{O}(D^3)$ matrix-free solvers, accurately reproducing the exact ground-state energies and Conformal Field Theory (CFT) entropy profiles of the Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg chain, as well as the non-local topological string order of the Spin-$1$ AKLT model. To demonstrate universal reproducibility, we tested a $4 \times 4$ grid of state-of-the-art foundation models (GPT, Claude, Gemini, and Kimi/Kimi Agent) for the LLM-1 and LLM-2 stages, achieving a 100\% (16/16) success rate. Remarkably, the end-to-end development of each verified physics engine required less than 24 hours of wall-clock time, comprising approximately 14 hours of active human-AI collaboration. This framework provides a model-agnostic, highly reproducible blueprint for radically accelerating software development across the physical sciences.
\end{abstract}

\maketitle

% =========================================================
\section{Introduction}
\label{sec:intro}
% =========================================================

The numerical simulation of strongly correlated quantum many-body systems has been fundamentally transformed by the advent of tensor network methods. In one spatial dimension, the Density-Matrix Renormalization Group (DMRG) algorithm \cite{White1992, Schollwock2011} and the associated Matrix Product State (MPS) formalism provide an essentially exact description of both critical and gapped quantum phases. However, translating the continuous, analytical mathematics of tensor network theory into discrete, high-performance software remains a formidable challenge. 

Developing a production-ready DMRG codebase from scratch traditionally requires months of dedicated graduate-level effort. The difficulty lies not in the conceptual physics, but in the stringent demands of computational implementation: researchers must meticulously track multi-dimensional array indices during tensor contractions (e.g., via \texttt{numpy.einsum}), manage gauge degrees of freedom to maintain canonical forms, and bypass $\mathcal{O}(D^4)$ memory blowups by designing matrix-free iterative eigensolvers (where $D$ is the virtual bond dimension). Consequently, there is a steep learning curve that bottlenecks the rapid prototyping of new algorithms and tensor geometries.

Simultaneously, the rapid advancement of Large Language Models (LLMs) has revolutionized general-purpose software engineering. Foundation models can now generate, debug, and refactor code for web development and data science with remarkable proficiency. Yet, when applied to computational physics, these models frequently stumble. Zero-shot attempts to generate tensor network algorithms directly from theoretical literature typically result in ``hallucinatory'' code. Because LLMs lack intrinsic spatial reasoning, they frequently output mismatched tensor legs, fail to distinguish between complex conjugation and conjugate transposition, or propose naive dense-matrix contractions that instantly exhaust system memory. Thus, a direct ``Paper to Program'' translation using isolated AI prompts is currently unviable for advanced scientific computing.

In this work, we propose a solution to this bottleneck: a multi-stage, Human-in-the-Loop (HITL) workflow that conceptualizes the AI agents not as monolithic code-generators, but as a ``Virtual Research Group.'' By dividing the development process into specialized roles—a Junior Theorist (LLM-0) for literature extraction, a Senior Postdoc (LLM-1) for formal specification, and a Research Assistant (LLM-2) for code generation—we demonstrate that the reliability of AI-assisted programming can be drastically improved. 

The core innovation of our methodology is the introduction of the \textit{Intermediate Technical Specification}. We demonstrate that by forcing the workflow to pause and generate a mathematically rigorous LaTeX blueprint (enforcing universal index conventions and matrix-free logic), the variance and hallucination rates of the coding LLM are effectively eliminated. The mathematical LaTeX acts as a ``Universal API,'' transforming a highly complex physics-reasoning task into a strictly constrained syntax-translation task.

To benchmark this workflow, we generated a complete, object-oriented MPS and DMRG engine in Python. We demonstrate its physical exactness by successfully resolving the critical scaling of the Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg model and the hidden topological order of the Spin-$1$ Affleck-Kennedy-Lieb-Tasaki (AKLT) model. Furthermore, we rigorously prove the reproducibility of this method by testing it across a $4 \times 4$ grid of modern foundation models (Kimi, Gemini, GPT, and Claude), achieving a 100\% (16 out of 16) success rate. Most significantly, this multi-agent workflow compressed a traditional months-long development cycle into less than 24 hours of wall-clock time ($\sim 14$ active hours), establishing a highly reproducible paradigm for accelerating scientific software development.

% =========================================================
\section{Methodology: The ``Virtual Research Group'' Workflow}
\label{sec:methodology}
% =========================================================

Translating continuous quantum many-body theory into discrete, high-performance array operations requires tacit computational knowledge that is rarely explicit in physical literature. To navigate this, we structured our multi-agent LLM pipeline to mimic the pedagogical and hierarchical dynamics of a traditional academic research group. As illustrated in Fig.~\ref{fig:workflow}, the workflow partitions algorithm development into three distinct LLM roles, supervised by a human Principal Investigator (PI).

\begin{figure*}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth]{figure1.png} 
    \caption{\textbf{The ``Paper to Program'' Multi-Agent Workflow.} The development process mimics a virtual research group. \textbf{(A)} The theoretical source material. \textbf{(B)} A zero-shot translation by LLM-0 (``Junior Theorist'') yields a flawed initial draft, characterized by hallucinated tensor indices and severe memory scaling bottlenecks. \textbf{(C)} The crucial intermediate step: LLM-1 (``Senior Postdoc'') reviews and corrects the draft, generating a mathematically rigorous formal specification. This stage enforces universal index conventions and explicit memory optimizations (e.g., \texttt{optimize=True}). \textbf{(D)} Constrained by this strict blueprint, LLM-2 (``Coder'') reliably generates scalable Python code. The Human PI remains in the loop strictly for high-level physics verification and pedagogical feedback.}
    \label{fig:workflow}
\end{figure*}

\subsection{Stage 1: Theory Extraction (LLM-0 / The ``Junior Theorist'')}
The workflow begins by providing the source literature—in this case, the comprehensive review of MPS and DMRG by Schollw\"ock (2011)—to the first foundation model, LLM-0 (Kimi 2.5). The objective of this agent is to extract the fundamental theoretical equations, such as Matrix Product Operator (MPO) representations, QR/SVD canonicalization routines, and effective Hamiltonian contractions, and translate them into an initial LaTeX draft.

Much like a junior graduate student, LLM-0 succeeds at extracting the broad mathematical strokes but frequently fails to account for computational realities. As depicted in Fig.~\ref{fig:workflow}B, direct extraction often results in messy pseudo-code containing hallucinatory index mappings, missing library prefixes, and a failure to optimize tensor contraction paths, which would immediately lead to fatal $\mathcal{O}(D^6)$ memory blowups if executed.

\subsection{Stage 2: Expert Specification (LLM-1 / The ``Senior Postdoc'')}
The most critical innovation of this workflow is Stage 2. We expressly forbid the direct translation of the Stage 1 draft into Python. Instead, the draft is passed to an ``expert reviewer'' model, LLM-1 (Gemini 3.1), tasked with line-by-line verification for computational and coding readiness. 

LLM-1 acts as the senior specifying agent. It autonomously identifies algorithmic ambiguities and injects tacit physics domain knowledge to create a rigorous formal blueprint (Fig.~\ref{fig:workflow}C). Key optimizations introduced at this stage include:
\begin{itemize}
    \item \textbf{Universal Index Conventions:} Explicitly defining a rigid nomenclature for tensor legs (e.g., \texttt{b/B} for MPO bonds, \texttt{x/X} for bra bonds, \texttt{y/Y} for ket bonds) to entirely eliminate broadcasting errors.
    \item \textbf{Matrix-Free Scalability:} Replacing dense matrix constructions with iterative \texttt{scipy.sparse.linalg.LinearOperator} implementations, ensuring the effective Hamiltonian application scales strictly as $\mathcal{O}(D^3)$.
    \item \textbf{Memory Management:} Enforcing the use of \texttt{np.tensordot} for gauge shifting to leverage optimized BLAS routines, and differentiating between memory views and deep copies in NumPy array reshaping.
\end{itemize}
The output of this stage is a flawlessly compiled, highly detailed LaTeX document. This document serves as a universal, model-agnostic Application Programming Interface (API) for the final coding stage.

\subsection{Stage 3: Code Implementation \& HITL Mentorship (LLM-2 \& Human PI)}
In the final stage, the formal LaTeX specification is passed to the implementation agent, LLM-2. Because the LaTeX blueprint mathematically constrains the tensor shapes and contraction paths, LLM-2 is relieved of spatial reasoning tasks and operates purely as a syntax-translation engine, outputting object-oriented Python classes.

At this juncture, the human researcher acts as the Principal Investigator. Rather than writing boilerplate code or hunting for missing syntax, the Human-in-the-Loop (HITL) executes the Jupyter Notebook and evaluates physical observables. If an error occurs—for instance, if a two-site DMRG update collapses to an unphysical bond dimension of $D=1$—the PI does not rewrite the code. Instead, the PI provides \textit{pedagogical feedback} to LLM-2 (Fig.~\ref{fig:workflow}D), explaining the physical impossibility of the result. Prompted with this physics-based insight, the LLM autonomously deduces the flaw in its contraction wiring and rewrites the function correctly.

\begin{figure*}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth]{figure2.png} 
    \caption{\textbf{The Accelerated Development Timeline.} The complete workflow—from parsing the theoretical review paper to finalizing a verified, matrix-free DMRG codebase—was executed in under 24 hours of wall-clock time. This required approximately 14 hours of active, human-in-the-loop collaboration, representing a massive acceleration compared to traditional 3-to-6-month development cycles.}
    \label{fig:timeline}
\end{figure*}

% =========================================================
\section{Results: Systematic Reproducibility and Workflow Acceleration}
\label{sec:reproducibility}
% =========================================================

To rigorously evaluate the robustness of our multi-stage workflow, we conducted a systematic cross-compatibility test using a diverse set of state-of-the-art foundation models: Kimi, Gemini, GPT, and Claude. The initial theoretical extraction (LLM-0) was kept constant, while the models utilized for the expert specification (LLM-1) and the code implementation (LLM-2) were permuted to form a $4 \times 4$ testing grid.

\begin{table}[htbp]
    \centering

A checkmark ($\checkmark$) indicates successful physics verification. Notably, when Kimi was utilized for the code generation stage, the autonomous `Kimi Agent' framework was deployed.}
    \label{tab:reproducibility}
    \renewcommand{\arraystretch}{1.3}

        \textbf{Claude} & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ & $\checkmark$ \\

This perfect completion rate underscores a fundamental insight: the primary cause of failure in zero-shot code generation is not an inherent limitation in the reasoning capabilities of the models, but rather the absence of strict computational definitions. The rigorous LaTeX specification acts as a ``Universal API''—a model-agnostic blueprint that completely flattens the performance variance between different AI ecosystems (e.g., seamlessly bridging OpenAI's GPT with Moonshot AI's Kimi Agent).

Furthermore, despite the cross-model handoffs, the total development time for each of the 16 successful paths remained under 24 hours of wall-clock time (Fig.~\ref{fig:timeline}). On average, this corresponded to approximately 14 hours of active, human-in-the-loop collaboration—a reduction of several orders of magnitude compared to traditional tensor network software development.

% =========================================================
\section{Results: Physics Verification and Scalability}
\label{sec:physics_verification}
% =========================================================

To definitively prove that the AI-generated codebase is both scalable and physically exact, we benchmarked the \texttt{DMRGEngine} against two paradigmatic 1D quantum many-body systems: the Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg model (a critical phase) and the Spin-$1$ Affleck-Kennedy-Lieb-Tasaki (AKLT) model (a gapped topological phase). The codebase successfully executed matrix-free Hamiltonian applications in both cases, completely avoiding the $\mathcal{O}(d^2 D^4)$ memory bottlenecks that typically plague naive tensor network implementations.

\begin{figure*}[htbp]
    \centering
    \includegraphics[width=0.98\textwidth]{fig_physics.png} 
    \caption{\textbf{Physics Verification and Scaling Benchmarks.} The AI-generated codebase accurately captures the distinct physics of critical and gapped topological phases. \textbf{(a)} Ground state energy $E_0$ of the $L=12$ Heisenberg chain extrapolated against the inverse bond dimension $1/D$. \textbf{(b)} Finite-size scaling of the Heisenberg ground state energy density $E_0/L$ versus $1/L$, accurately extrapolating to the exact Bethe Ansatz thermodynamic limit ($e_\infty = -0.4431$). \textbf{(c)} Bipartite entanglement entropy profile for the $L=12$ Heisenberg chain, exhibiting expected even-odd boundary oscillations and matching the Conformal Field Theory (CFT) prediction for central charge $c=1$. \textbf{(d)} Ground state energy of the AKLT model perfectly matching the exact analytical formula $E_0 = -(L-1)\cdot 2/3$ across various system sizes. \textbf{(e)} Bond entanglement entropy for the AKLT model ($D=2$), showing the bulk bonds plateauing exactly at $\ln 2 \approx 0.6931$, reflecting the fractionalized virtual spin-$1/2$ singlets of the valence-bond solid state. \textbf{(f)} The non-local string order parameter perfectly plateauing at the theoretical value of $-4/9$, confirming the hidden topological order of the Haldane phase.}
    \label{fig:physics}
\end{figure*}

\subsection{Benchmark I: Criticality in the Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg Chain}
We first tested the algorithm on the isotropic Spin-$1/2$ Heisenberg chain with open boundary conditions (OBC). Critical 1D systems are notoriously difficult for MPS algorithms due to the logarithmic divergence of entanglement entropy, making them ideal stress tests for the generated code. 

As shown in Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}a, the generated code successfully implements bond-dimension scaling. For a system of size $L=12$, the ground-state energy $E_0$ converges smoothly as a function of the inverse bond dimension $1/D$. To verify the code's accuracy in the thermodynamic limit, we performed a finite-size scaling (FSS) analysis (Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}b). By plotting the energy density $E_0/L$ against $1/L$, we extrapolated the bulk energy density to $e_\infty = -0.4427$, in excellent agreement with the exact Bethe Ansatz value of $-0.4431$.

Furthermore, the code was utilized to extract the bipartite entanglement entropy $S$ across the chain (Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}c). The generated engine accurately resolved both the prominent even-odd Friedel oscillations induced by the open boundaries and the overarching logarithmic scaling profile predicted by Conformal Field Theory (CFT) for a Tomonaga-Luttinger liquid with central charge $c=1$.

\subsection{Benchmark II: Topological Order in the Spin-$1$ AKLT Model}
To test the engine's ability to capture complex non-local physics and gapped phases, we evaluated the Spin-$1$ AKLT model. The AKLT ground state is a symmetry-protected topological (SPT) phase, characterized by fractionalized edge spins and a hidden string order, which can be exactly represented by an MPS of bond dimension $D=2$.

The generated code successfully constructed the exact $D_W = 14$ Matrix Product Operator (MPO) for the AKLT biquadratic interaction. As demonstrated in Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}d, the DMRG-computed ground state energies precisely matched the analytical formula $E_0 = -(L-1) \cdot 2/3$ across multiple system sizes. 

The physical fidelity of the tensor network is further proven by the entanglement spectrum (Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}e). The code accurately resolved the bulk bond entanglement entropy plateauing at exactly $\ln 2 \approx 0.6931$. This perfectly reflects the underlying valence-bond solid (VBS) picture, where cutting any bulk bond severs exactly one maximally entangled virtual spin-$1/2$ singlet.

Finally, we prompted the implementation agent to calculate the non-local string order parameter:
\begin{equation}
    \mathcal{O}_{\text{string}}(i_0, i_0+r) = \langle S_{i_0}^z \exp\left(i\pi \sum_{k=i_0+1}^{i_0+r-1} S_k^z\right) S_{i_0+r}^z \rangle
\end{equation}
As shown in Fig.~\ref{fig:physics}f, the generated code flawlessly executed this multi-site string operator contraction. The correlator exhibits a perfectly flat plateau at exactly $-4/9$, the theoretical signature of the AKLT phase. This confirms that the multi-stage LLM workflow is capable of generating codebase architectures sophisticated enough to probe both critical entanglement scaling and advanced topological properties in quantum many-body systems.

% =========================================================
\section{Discussion and Conclusion}
\label{sec:discussion}
% =========================================================

The integration of Large Language Models into scientific computing promises to radically accelerate the pace of research, yet zero-shot code generation consistently fails when applied to advanced quantum many-body algorithms. In this work, we demonstrated that the bottleneck is not an inherent limitation in the reasoning capabilities of modern foundation models, but rather a failure of workflow architecture. By structuring the AI agents as a ``Virtual Research Group''—separating the tasks of theory extraction, rigorous mathematical specification, and syntax implementation—we successfully bridged the gap between abstract theoretical literature and scalable, production-ready software.

The central innovation of this methodology is the introduction of the intermediate LaTeX specification (LLM-1). We found that providing a coding agent (LLM-2) with a raw, unoptimized extraction of physical equations inevitably leads to fatal spatial reasoning errors, such as hallucinated tensor contractions and unmitigated $\mathcal{O}(D^6)$ memory blowups. However, by forcing an ``expert reviewer'' agent to first generate a mathematically airtight LaTeX blueprint, the final coding task is reduced to a highly constrained syntax translation. The mathematical LaTeX acts as a ``Universal API,'' flawlessly coordinating different AI ecosystems and yielding a 100\% (16/16) cross-model reproducibility rate.

Furthermore, this workflow redefines the role of the computational physicist. The Human-in-the-Loop is elevated from writing boilerplate syntax to acting as a Principal Investigator. Instructed purely on physical logic (e.g., correcting unphysical state collapses), the AI autonomously diagnosed its contraction wiring errors and implemented the correct algorithmic structure. Most remarkably, this entire development cycle was completed in under 24 hours of wall-clock time, compressing the traditional software development timeline by several orders of magnitude. 

Looking forward, this multi-stage, specification-driven workflow is highly generalizable. We anticipate that this paradigm can be immediately applied to accelerate the development of even more complex quantum algorithms, such as Time-Dependent Variational Principle (TDVP) engines, infinite-system DMRG (iDMRG), and 2D tensor network frameworks like Projected Entangled Pair States (PEPS). By effectively eliminating the coding and debugging bottleneck, this approach frees researchers to focus entirely on algorithmic design and physical discovery.

% =========================================================

\end{acknowledgments}
```
